So numbers of the form 2*3^e for e>=2 work.

Also numbers of the form 2*3^e * p? Possibly for p large, we get similar fixed points. Let's explore general structure.

We want to find all a_1 such that the sequence is infinite. That means we never hit a number with <3 proper divisors. It might be that the sequence is eventually periodic with all terms having at least 3 proper divisors. Possibly the only way to avoid primes and squares of primes is to land on a fixed point that is "self-sum". But could there be cycles of length >1? For example, could there be a 2-cycle: N -> M -> N, with both N and M having at least 3 proper divisors? Let's try to see if any such cycle exists. For small numbers, we can compute f(N) for N up to maybe 200 and see if any cycle appears.

But note: f(N) is the sum of three largest proper divisors. For N > 1, the largest proper divisor is at most N-1 (if N is composite, the largest proper divisor is N/p where p is the smallest prime factor; for N composite, p >= 2, so N/p <= N/2). So f(N) is at most N/2 + N/3 + N/4? Actually we need the three largest proper divisors; they are all at most N/2. So f(N) <= (N/2 + N/3 + N/4) = (6N+4N+3N)/12 = 13N/12, which is > N. But actually the three largest could be N/2, N/3, N/4 if the number has small prime factors? For example, N=12, N/2=6, N/3=4, N/4=3, sum=13 >12. For N=30, N/2=15, N/3=10, N/5=6? Actually 30/5=6, sum=31 >30. So f(N) can exceed N. But also can be less than N. So the sequence could increase or decrease.

But importantly, if the sequence ever reaches a prime, it stops. So to be infinite, the sequence must avoid primes. Also avoid numbers with exactly two proper divisors (i.e., p^2). Could it ever reach a number with exactly two proper divisors? For example, 9 has only proper divisors 1,3 (two). So if we get 9, then a_{n+1} = 3+1=4? Actually careful: For a number with exactly two proper divisors, the sum of the three largest proper divisors is not defined because there are only two proper divisors. But the problem says the infinite sequence consists of positive integers each of which has at least three proper divisors. So the sequence cannot contain any number with fewer than three proper divisors. So if at any step we get a number with exactly two proper divisors, the definition of the next term would require summing three largest proper divisors, but there are only two; that would be undefined. So such a sequence would not be infinite. Therefore, every term must have at least three proper divisors. So we must avoid numbers with tau(N) = 2 or 3 (since tau=2 means prime, tau=3 means p^2). So tau(N) >= 4.

Now, we need to find all a_1 such that the entire infinite sequence stays in the set of numbers with tau(N) >= 4. That seems like a very restrictive condition. Possibly the answer is that a_1 must be a multiple of 6? But 12 is a multiple of 6 and fails. So not that.

Maybe we can prove that the sequence eventually becomes constant at 6, 18, 42, 54, 66, 78, 102, 108, etc. But note: 6 is fixed, 18 fixed, 42 fixed, 54 fixed, 66 fixed, 78 fixed, 102 fixed, etc. So many numbers are fixed points. But are there any numbers that lead to a cycle of length >1? Let's try some numbers that are not fixed points but might cycle. For instance, consider N=24: f(24)=26, f(26)=16, f(16)=14, f(14)=10, f(10)=8, f(8)=7, f(7)=? 7 prime, fails. So no cycle. N=36: f(36)=39, f(39)=17, prime. So no. N=60: f(60)=65, f(65)=19, prime. Thus perhaps the only numbers that have infinite trajectories are those that eventually reach a fixed point, and those fixed points are exactly numbers N such that S(N)=N. But we also need to ensure that every term in the sequence has at least three proper divisors. If the sequence becomes constant at a fixed point, that's fine. But if it cycles, we need to check that all cycle terms have tau>=4. Could there be a 2-cycle? Let's analyze possible cycles.

Suppose N and M are two distinct numbers such that f(N)=M and f(M)=N, with both having tau>=4. Then N and M are both composite with at least 3 proper divisors. Also note that f(N) is the sum of three largest proper divisors of N. Since the three largest proper divisors are at most N/2, N/3, N/4, we have f(N) <= 13N/12. So if N < M, then N <= (12/13)M, so M is at least 13N/12 > N. So it's possible that one is larger than the other. In a 2-cycle, we would have N < M and M < N? Actually if f(N)=M and f(M)=N, then either N=M (fixed point) or N<M and M<N which is impossible, so they must be equal. But careful: f(N) could be less than N, and f(M) could be greater than M, but then we could have N > M and M > N? Actually if N > M, then f(N) could be less than N, and f(M) could be greater than M, but that doesn't directly give a contradiction. However, if f(N)=M and f(M)=N, then applying f again, we get f(f(N))=N. So N is a periodic point of period 2. That is possible even if N != M. For example, could there be numbers such that f(N)=M and f(M)=N with N != M? That would imply that N and M are distinct and each maps to the other. That is a 2-cycle. Then we would have f(N)=M and f(M)=N. Consider the size. If N < M, then f(N)=M > N, so f(N) > N. And f(M)=N < M, so f(M) < M. So it's possible that one number increases and the other decreases. For a 2-cycle, we need f(N)=M and f(M)=N. This means that M = sum of three largest proper divisors of N, and N = sum of three largest proper divisors of M. So we need two numbers that are each other's "three-largest-sum". This is analogous to amicable numbers but with three largest divisors. We need to see if any such pair exists. Let's try to find small examples.

Thus the possible a_1 are exactly: 72 and all numbers N such that S(N)=N. But we need to determine the exact description of those N.

We have characterized S(N)=N as: N is composite and the sum of its three largest proper divisors equals N. Let's characterize all such N.

We need to find all N with S(N)=N. Let's denote the proper divisors in decreasing order: d1 > d2 > d3 >= ... . Then d1 = N/p1 where p1 is the smallest prime factor. Also, d2 and d3 are the next largest proper divisors. As argued, the three largest proper divisors correspond to N divided by the three smallest divisors >1. Let the three smallest divisors >1 be s1 < s2 < s3. Then d1 = N/s1, d2 = N/s2, d3 = N/s3. So S(N) = N*(1/s1+1/s2+1/s3). The condition S(N)=N gives 1/s1+1/s2+1/s3 = 1.

Thus we need to find all integers N such that if we list the divisors of N greater than 1 in increasing order, the three smallest are s1,s2,s3 and satisfy 1/s1+1/s2+1/s3=1.

Note that s1 is the smallest divisor >1, so s1 is the smallest prime factor of N. So s1 >= 2. Then 1/s1 <= 1/2. To sum to 1, we need 1/s2+1/s3 = 1 - 1/s1. Since s2 and s3 are at least s1+1? Actually they are divisors, so s2 >= s1+1? Not necessarily; if s1=2, s2 could be 3 or 4, etc. So we need to find all triples of positive integers (s1,s2,s3) with s1 < s2 < s3, all divisors of N (with s1 being the smallest >1), such that 1/s1+1/s2+1/s3=1. And then N must be such that these are indeed the three smallest divisors >1. That imposes constraints on the prime factorization.

Let's find all integer solutions to 1/a+1/b+1/c=1 with a<b<c positive integers. This is a classic Diophantine equation. The possible solutions are:

- (2,3,6): 1/2+1/3+1/6=1.
- (2,4,4): 1/2+1/4+1/4=1, but b=c not allowed because we need strict inequality? Actually we need s2 < s3, so (2,4,4) has b=c, so not allowed because divisors are distinct? So f(N) can be less than N. For N=12, s1=2,s2=3,s3=4? Actually 12=2^2*3, divisors:2,3,4,6,12. So s1=2,s2=3,s3=4, f=6+4+3=13 >12. So the sign depends on s3.

Similarly, if N is divisible by 3 but not by 2, then s1=3, s2=9? Or s2=5? Actually if N is divisible by 3 and not by 2, it could be 3^k * p where p is an odd prime. The smallest divisor >1 is 3. Then the second smallest divisor is either 9 if 3^2|N, or p if p<9 and p|N. So f(N) = N/3 + N/s2 + N/s3. This could be > N or < N. For N=15, s1=3,s2=5,s3=15? Actually 15=3*5, divisors:3,5,15. So s1=3,s2=5,s3=15, but then f=5+3+1=9? Wait careful: proper divisors of 15: 1,3,5. The three largest proper divisors: 5,3,1 sum=9. So f=9, which is less than 15. So f(N) can be less than N.

But we need to show that any infinite sequence must eventually become constant at a fixed point. That is, the only possible infinite sequences are those that are eventually constant at a number in A. And we already know that if a_1 ∈ A, then it's constant. If a_1 = 72, then it goes to 78 ∈ A and then constant. So the only possible a_1 are those that either are in A or map to A in one step. And we need to prove that there are no other numbers that map to A in one step besides 72, and that no number maps to 72. So the set of possible a_1 is A ∪ {72}.

Now, we need to produce a rigorous proof.

Let's start with some lemmas.

Lemma 1: For any positive integer N, if N has at least three proper divisors, then f(N) = N * (1/s1 + 1/s2 + 1/s3), where s1 < s2 < s3 are the three smallest divisors of N greater than 1.

Proof: The proper divisors of N are all numbers d such that d|N and 1 ≤ d < N. The largest proper divisor is N divided by the smallest divisor greater than 1, which is s1. Similarly, the second largest is N/s2, and the third largest is N/s3, because the mapping d → N/d is order-reversing, and the three smallest divisors greater than 1 correspond to the three largest proper divisors. Need to ensure that there are at least three proper divisors, so s3 exists. Since N has at least three proper divisors, it has at least four divisors total, so τ(N) ≥ 4, which means there are at least three divisors greater than 1. So s1,s2,s3 exist. Then f(N)=N/s1+N/s2+N/s3.

Lemma 2: If N is divisible by 2 and 3, then s1=2, s2=3, and s3=6 if and only if N is not divisible by 4 or 5. Moreover, in that case f(N)=N.

Proof: Since 2|N and 3|N, the divisors greater than 1 include 2,3,6. The three smallest divisors >1 are the smallest three numbers in the divisor set. The only candidates for numbers less than 6 are 4 and 5. If 4|N, then 4 is a divisor and 4<6, so s3 would be 4, not 6. Similarly, if 5|N, then 5 is a divisor and 5<6, so s3 would be 5. So if N is not divisible by 4 or 5, then the three smallest divisors are exactly 2,3,6. Therefore, f(N)=N/2+N/3+N/6 = N. Conversely, if f(N)=N, then 1/s1+1/s2+1/s3=1. Since s1=2 (smallest prime factor), we must have s1=2 (otherwise if s1≥3, then 1/3+1/4+1/5=0.783, not 1). Actually we can argue: The only solution in positive integers with a<b<c to 1/a+1/b+1/c=1 is (2,3,6). So s1=2, s2=3, s3=6. Therefore, N is divisible by 2 and 3, and not divisible by 4 or 5. So Lemma 2 characterizes fixed points.

Thus, A = { N : 6|N, 4∤N, 5∤N }.

Now, we need to analyze the dynamics. Let's denote the sequence a_n. We know that each a_n must have τ ≥ 4.

Observation: If a_n ∈ A, then a_{n+1}=a_n, so the sequence becomes constant.

Thus, the only way to have an infinite sequence that is not eventually constant is if the sequence never lands in A, but then it must avoid primes and squares of primes. However, we suspect that the only numbers that can avoid primes and squares of primes are those that eventually land in A. So we need to prove that if a_1 ∉ A, then either a_2 is prime or a square of a prime, or the sequence eventually hits such a number, unless a_1=72.

But we need to be careful: a_1=72 is not in A, but its orbit is 72,78,78,... So it does not hit a prime or square of prime. So we need to prove that 72 is the only such number.

We can try to prove that for any N not in A, f(N) is either prime or a square of a prime, or f(N) is in A, and if f(N) is in A, then N must be 72. But we already have examples like N=24 gives f=26, which is not prime or square of prime (26 is composite, not a square of prime). So the statement "f(N) is prime or square of prime" is false. So we need a different approach.

Maybe we can consider the "largest proper divisor" d1(N). Note that if N is even, d1(N) = N/2. If N is odd, d1(N) = N/p where p is the smallest prime factor. Since N has at least three proper divisors, it is composite. Let's denote p = s1. Then d1 = N/p. The sum f(N) = N/p + N/s2 + N/s3. Since s2 and s3 are at least s1+1? But they are divisors, so they can be s1^2 if s1^2 divides N, or other primes.

We can try to bound f(N) in terms of N and the smallest prime factor. For N even, p=2, so f(N) = N/2 + N/s2 + N/s3. Since s2 ≥ 3 (if N is divisible by 3) or s2 ≥ 4 (if N is divisible by 4). In any case, s2 ≥ 3. And s3 ≥ 5? Actually if s2=3, then s3 could be 4? But if 4|N, then s3=4? Wait if N is divisible by 2 and 3 and 4, then s1=2, s2=3, s3=4? Actually divisors: 2,3,4,6,... so s3=4. So s3 can be 4. So we need to consider cases based on the exponent of 2 and the presence of 3.

Maybe we can prove that if N is not in A, then the sequence eventually strictly decreases until it hits a number with fewer than 3 proper divisors. But we have examples like 12->13 (increase), 24->26 (increase), 30->31 (increase), 36->39 (increase), 40->38 (decrease), 48->52 (increase), 56->50 (decrease), etc. So the sequence is not monotonic. However, we can analyze the behavior of the "three largest proper divisors" and see that if N is not in A, then either N is divisible by 4 or 5, or N is divisible by 2 but not by 3, or N is divisible by 3 but not by 2, or N is odd composite with no small primes. In all these cases, we might be able to show that after a few steps, we get a number that is either prime or a square of a prime. But we need to be systematic.

Now, we need to produce a rigorous proof that these are exactly the possible values. Let's outline the proof:

1. Define f(N) as sum of three largest proper divisors. Show that if N has at least three proper divisors, then f(N) = N/s1 + N/s2 + N/s3 where s1,s2,s3 are the three smallest divisors >1.

2. Show that if N is not divisible by 3, then f(N) < N. (Proof: For even N, s1=2, and s2 >= 4 if 4|N, or s2 >= 5 if N≡2 mod4; in either case, f(N) = N/2 + N/s2 + N/s3 ≤ N/2 + N/4 + N/5 = 19N/20 < N. For odd N, s1 is an odd prime p≥3, so f(N) ≤ N/3 + N/4 + N/5 = 47N/60 < N. Thus, for any N not divisible by 3, f(N) < N.)

3. Therefore, if a_1 is not divisible by 3, then the sequence a_n is strictly decreasing (since f(a_n) < a_n). Since the sequence consists of positive integers, it cannot be infinite unless it becomes constant, but strict decrease precludes that. However, we also need that each term has at least three proper divisors; the decreasing sequence must eventually reach a number with fewer than three proper divisors (i.e., a prime or a square of a prime), which would contradict the condition that each term has at least three proper divisors. Thus, a_1 must be divisible by 3. Also, a_1 must be even? Not necessarily, but if a_1 is odd and divisible by 3, then it's odd composite, and from step 2, f(a_1) < a_1, so the sequence decreases. Given that this is an IMO Shortlist problem, the answer is likely to be: all numbers of the form 6k where k is not divisible by 2 or 5, i.e., k is odd and not divisible by 5? But we saw that 72 works, and 72=6*12, k=12 is even. So that's not it. Also, 216 works (k=36 even). So it's more complex.

Maybe the intended solution uses a different approach: Show that the only possible infinite sequences are those that are eventually constant at a fixed point, and then characterize the fixed points.
